Write an introduction to the book that talks about the theme(s) you want to emphasize. Illustrate the theme(s) with specific passages. Some possibilities: immigration/migration; women in the Bible; foreigners in the Bible; human/divine faithfulness; etc." As someone who has occasionally dabbled in the fictional writing arts, it is safe to say that the mind behind the story of Ruth does something very special with this book. Placed up against the backdrop of books like the Exodus with its plagues and miracles, or Joshua with God sending the walls of a fortified city crumbling, the book of Ruth can seem quiet, even unimpressive. There's no burning bush, no trumpets, no fleece; there are only women trying to make their way in a world that is opposed to them doing so. The prophet Elijah might agree with Ruth, that after the whirlwind and the fire and the quakes, God is in the still, small voice that comes afterwards. Ruth's story is the still small voice. In his sermon on Ruth, Gaiser speaks of the critic-panned painting of Christ as a simple carpenter by John Everett Mills. "Where are the identifying halos and the appropriate robes? Where are the obligatory angels and the adoring shepherds? Is God in this picture at all?" (Albing, 170). We often want a bombastic God, an unmistakable Christ, the flaming tongues of Spirit, but in Ruth we get plain old people. However, this book, thematically, deals with subtlety. It has moments where the presence of God is assured, but you have to look for them. You must be intent on finding it, even ascribing action or happenstance to God in Ruth. And in that way, it is a spirituality more recognizable than any other in the Bible. (Unless your nightstand regularly bursts into non-devouring flame and orders you to remove your bunny slippers to stand on the sacred carpet, I'm guessing the spirituality you and I have dealt with most of our lives is of the subtle variety.) Novelists, too, trade in the currency of subtlety. They slide in motifs for careful readers, building to moments of crescendo for the one paying attention. Here's a few to note: 1.) The Kanaf: Boaz offers Ruth a blessing on their first meeting. In 2:12, he says that the Lord's wings (kanaf) will be where Ruth takes refuge. But it is Boaz's wings that she asks to spread over her in protection in 3:9. "spread your cloak (kanaf) over me." Boaz is the manner of God's wings spreading over Ruth. 2.) When Boaz first meets Ruth, he asks "to whom does this worker-girl belong?" She doesn't have a name, and throughout the book, she's consistently called daughter, Moabite, worker-girl, foreigner, widow, woman and more. As Davis puts it, Boaz asks "not "who is she?; but "Whose is she?" Yet, when he asks Ruth on the threshing floor who is there, she names herself "I am Ruth." And once she has her son, she loses the epithet "foreigner," though some may argue that she is displaced by Naomi in the end of her story. (Yee, 130). 3.) Boaz is narratively introduced to us as a "ish gibbor hayil" or a 'man of great substance.' When he sees what Ruth has done, and applies the same word "hayil" to her. "Naming her as a woman of hayil, he unwittingly identifies her as his own perfect match." (Davis, 169) 4.) Lastly, we see that Naomi, upon her return to Bethlehem, can see only her emptiness, even though Ruth stands at her side. However, in the end of the book, the women in her community recall her to herself, calling Ruth better than seven sons, and noting Naomi's arms full of Ruth's precious son. As noted in Dr. Shifferdecker's sermon, "In the end, it is not barley or wheat, but love that fills Naomi's emptiness, and love that did so from the beginning." (Albing, 186.) There are many of these subtle pairs in the story of Ruth, and we have to be watching for them, just as we must be watching for God's movement in our own lives and eager to see the author's fingers in the subtle workings. You said: Cn you remind me what the exhaustion levels are, love? ChatGPT said: ChatGPT Of course! Here are the effects of exhaustion levels in D&D 5e: Level 1: Disadvantage on Ability Checks Your character has a harder time with tasks that require skill or focus. Level 2: Speed Halved Your movement speed is reduced, making it harder to navigate the battlefield or keep up with allies. Level 3: Disadvantage on Attack Rolls and Saving Throws Your effectiveness in combat and your ability to resist effects or damage is severely hampered. Level 4: Hit Point Maximum Halved Your maximum HP is reduced by half, making you much more fragile. Level 5: Speed Reduced to 0 Your character cannot move, effectively immobilizing you. Level 6: Death At this level, the character succumbs to exhaustion and dies. Each level stacks, so the penalties get progressively worse. A long rest and one ration of food and water typically remove one level of exhaustion, though some magic or abilities can also help alleviate it. Structure and Design Material: Gnomish calendars are crafted from a combination of enchanted wood, crystal, and luminescent metals like aurabrite (a glowing material that mimics starlight). Magical Integration Star Alignments: Certain days are marked as "Illumine Days," when celestial alignments enhance magical properties. Johnson's She Who Is: The Mystery of God in Feminist Theological Discourse presents a groundbreaking feminist exploration of God within Christian theology. Johnson critiques the traditional patriarchal language and imagery that dominate Christianity, arguing that this limits the way believers understand and experience the divine. Instead, she proposes a more inclusive and life-giving way to speak about God, drawing on feminist insights and theology. Key Themes Feminist Critique of Patriarchal Theology Johnson argues that the traditional male-dominated language used to describe God (e.g., "Father," "Lord") perpetuates gender inequality and distorts the fullness of God's mystery. She critiques how these metaphors often exclude or marginalize women's experiences. Retrieving Female Imagery for God Johnson revisits biblical and theological traditions to uncover and re-center feminine imagery for God. She emphasizes symbols like "Shekinah" (God's dwelling presence), "Sophia" (Divine Wisdom), and nurturing metaphors like mothering and birthing, to provide a fuller, richer understanding of God. Naming God as "She Who Is" Drawing on Exodus 3:14 ("I Am Who Am"), Johnson reframes God as She Who Is, combining the scriptural roots of God's self-revelation with a feminist lens. This name affirms God's mystery and being while challenging restrictive gendered depictions. Relationality and the Trinity Johnson explores the Trinity through feminist theology, presenting God as deeply relational. She emphasizes the communal, inclusive nature of the Trinity, breaking away from hierarchical and patriarchal interpretations. Salvation and Women's Experience Johnson integrates women's lived experiences into the theological conversation about salvation. She underscores the liberative dimensions of God's love, especially for those marginalized by patriarchal systems. A Call for Transformation Johnsonâ€™s work is ultimately a call to reimagine theological language and practice. She encourages a shift towards inclusive, life-affirming ways of speaking about and relating to God. Significance Johnsonâ€™s work is both a critique and a constructive proposal for theology. She challenges the church to recognize how language shapes belief and practice, and she offers a way forward that honors both the mystery of God and the dignity of all people. Her feminist theological approach invites believers to encounter God as a liberating, nurturing, and deeply relational presence. Would you like me to expand on any specific aspect? Thomas Aquinas: Existence of God Key Points: Five Proofs of Godâ€™s Existence (Quinque Viae): Motion: Everything in motion must have been set in motion by something else.
